You have raised two concerns and asked that they be brought together: first, a caution against the now-familiar postureâ€”too often invoked since the Copenhagen interpretationâ€”of treating â€œunexplainableâ€ as a cue to shut down inquiry, almost a sneer at science; second, an appreciation of the significance of Jaegwon Kimâ€™s dogged theoretical pursuit. With these in mind, I quote Chris Niebauerâ€™s statements sentence by sentence and respond to each. The overall strategy is as follows. Niebauer leverages the limits of thought to claim the dispensability of theory; precisely at that juncture, Kimâ€™s analysis pushes in the opposite direction. The harder the problem, the more exacting our theoretical instruments must be. Once theory is abandoned, consciousness loses not only explanation and prediction, but even causal standing. Kimâ€™s conditional reductionism, his transcendental reduction argument, and his critique of downward causation collectively neutralize Niebauerâ€™s no-theory declaration.

â€œIn this video, I want to explore the possibility that maybe we don't even need a theory of consciousness.â€

The opening sentence lays bare the basic premise. Yet the conclusion â€œwe donâ€™t need a theoryâ€ does not follow directly from epistemic humility. That an existing theory of consciousness is incomplete differs categorically from the claim that theory itself is unnecessary. As Kim argues, if one forswears reduction, mental causation dissolves. Irreducible mental properties either become causally impotent or are relegated to at best epiphenomenal glosses. The real choice is whether to accept that outcomeâ€”or instead to secure causal standing through functional analysis and reduction, thereby enabling explanation and prediction.

â€œMaybe a theory of consciousness is something that has come out of a culture of modern humans, that worships thoughts, worships thinking, considers intellectualizing the pious form of humanity.â€

Cultural critique does not substitute for the methodological requirements of consciousness research. When one confuses the pathology of â€œthought-worshipâ€ with the necessity of scientific theory, public judgment and clinical practice begin to crumble. Depth of anesthesia, the detection of consciousness in disorders of consciousness, pain assessment, and the analysis of sleepâ€“wake disorders cannot be replaced by experientialist testimony. In Kimâ€™s terms, theory is not a â€œdogmaâ€ but the minimal apparatus for causal explanation; without it, one cannot sustain the causal status of mental phenomena.

â€œAnd, of course, if you're into Zen, if you've explored the no-self experience, then it's absolutely obvious that all of these illusions are tied to intellectualism.â€

It may be granted that a no-self experience can loosen our grip on compulsive ratiocination. But a transformation in experience does not license the abolition of theory. Even Zen bequeathed extensive doctrinal and normative corpora. â€œTheorylessnessâ€ may be a posture for personal practice; it cannot anchor a public knowledge system. A theory of consciousness does not impose moral oughts; it seeks to render phenomena, causation, and prediction coherent. Here Niebauer conflates normativity with ontology.

â€œAnd so, let's start off with a few quotes from the Surgadatamâ€¦ Until I met my guru, I knew so many things. Now, I know nothing. The ultimate understanding is that there is no ultimate understanding.â€

Appeals to the guru evoke a stance toward life, not an ontology. â€œNot-knowingâ€ can be an admirable existential insight; it does not entail the dispensability of scientific theory. As Kim emphasizes, a gap in explanation is not a warrant to abandon theory; it is a signal to pursue functional analysis and reduction. When an explanatory gap is converted into an ethic, we have silently switched games.

â€œHe's an excellent way to dissolve the illusion of the supremacy of thought.â€

Exposing the illusion of thoughtâ€™s â€œsupremacyâ€ does not require jettisoning the instrumentality of thought. Consciousness is not intractable because we think too much, but because we lack the right tools of analysis. Only through functional characterization, a search for realizers, and multi-level explanatory schemes can we attain prediction and controllability. This maps onto Kimâ€™s three-step logic of reduction: functionalizing the target property; identifying its realizers in the base; and formulating a mechanism by which those realizers perform the function.

â€œWhen thought is devaluedâ€¦ Something maybe that's useful 30 minutes a day or so.â€

Treating thinking as a thirty-minute daily utility is a personal regimen, not a research method. Indeed, the local downgrading of thought runs counter to the demands of consciousness science, which must weld report, measurement, and intervention. That welding requires ongoing conceptual engineering and model revision. For precisely this linkage, Kim argues for functional reduction, since type-identity proclamations alone cannot close explanatory gaps.

â€œThe voice in the headâ€¦ symbols about reality tied in with the survival mechanism.â€

To reduce inner speech to a survival mechanism is, ironically, already to theorize. Binding symbols to survival is an explanatory, causal hypothesis; refining such claims is the business of theory. If theory were unnecessary, why offer this explanation at all? The rhetoric and the content subvert each other.

â€œAnd so, why create a theory of consciousness at all? â€¦ the character Chris had always desiredâ€¦ to create theoryâ€¦ But this is slowly dissolvedâ€¦ a theory isn't even necessary. What is necessary is to see that a theory isn't necessary.â€

At this point, the argument collapses into a circle. â€œWhat is necessary is to see that a theory isnâ€™t necessaryâ€ amounts to positing a higher-order theory of theoryâ€™s dispensability. As Kim distinguishes, reduction and reductive explanation are not identical, but each presupposes explanatory scaffolding. If we disallow all such scaffolding, what remains are first-person reportsâ€”yet public warrant and causal attributions for such reports again demand theoretical anchors.

â€œbasic, direct, conscious experiences, like the Pita Hanâ€¦ Why is there a need to explain it? â€¦ thereâ€™s no need to explain anythingâ€¦ just, this place is beautiful.â€

First, the ethnonym seems wrong; presumably the PirahÃ£ are intended. Second, debates around the PirahÃ£ already show how fraught the interpretation of language and culture can be; none of this justifies the leap â€œrefusal to explain = philosophical truth.â€ Third, elevating an ethnographic disposition (â€œwe do not explainâ€) into an epistemological maxim (â€œthere is no need to explainâ€) romanticizes constructivism. To find a landscape beautiful is one thing; to chart the state space of consciousness is another. In Kimâ€™s framework, the former is phenomenological description; the latter concerns functionalization and the search for realizers.

â€œquantum theory predicts really well, but it doesn't explain, no one seems to understand it.â€

Prediction without full understanding is a familiar theme in the philosophy of science, but it does not entail â€œno need for theory.â€ Moreover, the subsequent glossâ€”treating â€œquantumâ€ as meaning â€œindivisible, hence not analyzableâ€â€”is simply incorrect. Quantum refers to discreteness or minimal quanta; it does not mean â€œindecomposable.â€ A misplaced scientific metaphor cannot underwrite a no-theory manifesto.

â€œFreudian theoryâ€¦ great at explainingâ€¦ but it doesn't make any good predictions.â€

The critique of classical Freudian theoryâ€™s weak falsifiability has merit. But to infer the uselessness of theory in general from the failures of one theoretical family is a non sequitur. Kim warns precisely against such overgeneralization. The shortcomings of one approach do not undercut the standing of reduction and functional analysis as alternative routes.

â€œin neuroscience, theory is mostly pointing to different brain areasâ€¦ that might be satisfactory, but in others, it isn't. And it comes down to the limitations of thought.â€

Everyone acknowledges the limits of simple localization. But contemporary neuroscience pivots on functional networks, computational models, predictive processing, and causal intervention (e.g., stimulation, inhibition, prosthetic coupling). Brushing aside methodological advances under the blanket heading â€œlimitations of thoughtâ€ obscures the domain of public explanation and practical control. In Kimâ€™s taxonomy, the live options are neither bare type-identity nor indiscriminate nonreductionism; they are functionalization and a theory of realizers.

â€œwe talk one word at a timeâ€¦ thinking and language are connectedâ€¦ Chomskyâ€¦ we don't use language to communicateâ€¦ we use language to thinkâ€¦ you can think one thought at a time.â€

The pronouncements are bold but oversimplify the science. While verbal working memory and linguistic inference can be partly serial, perception, affect, and motor control exhibit massive parallelism. Mapping seriality onto â€œthought as a whole,â€ and simultaneity onto â€œexperience as a whole,â€ ignores a dense body of evidence in cognitive science. And the Chomskyan summary is contested. No such simplifications yield the conclusion that theory is unnecessary.

â€œI used to love working on watchesâ€¦ I can only think about one part at a time, but reality happens all at once.â€

The difficulty of decomposition is conceded; indeed, it motivates system-level modeling and numerical simulation. If theory cannot bridge partâ€“whole gaps, first-person testimony surely cannot. As Kim notes, to translate the intuition that â€œthe whole occurs at onceâ€ into a scientific idiom, one must again pursue functionalization, the search for realizers, and mechanisms by which those realizers do their work.

â€œthe left brain interpreter wants to break it downâ€¦ it's not dissectable. It is in itself a quantum. And that's all the word quantum means, is it can't be dissected.â€

The left-brain interpreter is a useful trope within a specific split-brain literature; it is overextended as a global portrait of everyday brain function. And again, â€œquantum = not dissectableâ€ is a conceptual error. Arguments built on a mistaken definition cannot bear the theoretical weight assigned to them.

â€œthe Mitsu problemâ€¦ medical doctorsâ€¦ focus on just one partâ€¦ even that turns out to be too complex for the thinking mind to understand.â€

In medicine, focusing on parts is a strategic divide-and-conquer, not a confession of theoretical bankruptcy. Kim translates partâ€“whole tensions into the language of levels and realization. A higher-level property E is realized in a lower-level base B; the causal powers of an instance of E are inherited from an instance of its realizer in B. This inheritance principle is what allows science to proceed toward design and intervention.

â€œusing the thinking mind to understand anything, maybe futile, in particular, completely futile when it comes to consciousness.â€

If thinking is futile in general, this very sentence is futile. Here experientialism runs into a performative contradiction. Kim presses further: nonreductionism is seductive, but if one wishes to preserve mental causation, one is driven toward reduction or at least functional reduction; otherwise, higher-level properties become causally idle.

â€œif you ever became a parentâ€¦ what to expectâ€¦ when you get on the No Self Tripâ€¦ devaluing thinkingâ€¦ a devaluing of the intellectual, a devaluing of theories.â€

During life transitions, theory may feel less absorbing. But a decline in â€œinterestâ€ is not a loss of warrant. In domains of public judgmentâ€”ethics, policy, clinical careâ€”the metric is responsibility, not amusement. As Kim cautions, remove theory and explanation and prediction collapse; mental causation becomes hollow.

â€œyou start working on left brain interpretationsâ€¦ they're not really what I thought they would beâ€¦ that ends up being the entirety of the intellectual world. It's just not entertaining anymore.â€

Reducing the intellectual world to an entertainment index is precisely the problem. Entertainment is not a surrogate for truth. Philosophy and science are public goods that cannot be canceled for lack of â€œfun.â€ And the very description offered here performs the so-called left-brain interpretation. The rhetoric refutes itself.

â€œyour attention moves from theorizingâ€¦ to direct experiences themselvesâ€¦ realizing there's really nothing else that needs to be done.â€

As a personal practice, such a conclusion can be respected. As a stance toward the public task of understanding consciousness, it yields the opposite prescription. What is needed now is not â€œnothing,â€ but the construction of theories that preserve phenomenal fidelity while improving controllability and predictive power. Kimâ€™s pathwayâ€”functionalizing the target property, identifying realizers, and building mechanismâ€”is the scaffolding. Reject that scaffolding, and the causal role of the higher-level property risks being preempted by its base-level causes.

Let me summarize Kimâ€™s core points in direct terms. First, if one rejects reduction, mental causes either lose causal status or are always substitutable by base-level causes. The supervenience-based argument presses nonreductive physicalism on the difficulty of accommodating mental causation. Second, reduction is not a mere identity slogan; it requires functional analysis. To proclaim that pain is â€œidenticalâ€ to C-fiber firing is not to explain but to erase the need for explanation. One must specify the functional role and then show how realizers execute that role through a mechanism. Third, to speak of emergence typically invites downward causation; yet simultaneous downward causation tends to violate noncircularity and the principle of causal efficacy. At best, one can defend temporally lagged, non-self-referential downward influence; even there, the risk remains that higher-level causes are causally preempted by base-level causes.

As for your second concernâ€”â€œWould someone like Jaegwon Kim have persisted in research out of ignorance?â€â€”the answer is unequivocal. He persisted not because he missed the implications of the hard problem, but because he recognized them. Precisely those implications drove him to the conclusion that more exacting theory was needed. For him, a hard problem is not a cue to desist; it is the reason to pursue functional reduction. Hence, where type identity fails to explain, he insists on the sequence â€œfunctionalization â†’ realizers â†’ mechanism,â€ while relentlessly targeting the causal gap that nonreductionism bequeaths.

Finally, let us separate norm and truth. Niebauer repeatedly invokes the â€œunknowabilityâ€ of the hard problem to smuggle in spiritual injunctions; but this problem is not a matter of ought. As Bernardo Kastrup notes, the debate over consciousness concerns our most fundamental ontological claimsâ€”what is more basic than what. Such claims are to be explored by science and logic. The moment we exchange ontological theses for ethical or practice-oriented exhortations, the debate exits philosophy and enters the self-help aisle. A thin homily cannot replace a tradition of disciplined scholarship. If things were otherwise, Fields Medalists would wake from meditation and declare in chorus, â€œTheory is unnecessary.â€ The reality is the reverse: they devote a lifetime not to discarding theory but to forging theories that are deeper and more concise. Somewhere in a quiet New York office, an editor might write: â€œSome close the shop when explanations stall. Scientists, on meeting a wall, count the bricks, scratch the mortar, and peer into the cavity beyond. Leaps are welcomeâ€”so long as they are launched from a firm footing.â€

## 1. êµìœ¡ì„ ë§¤ê°œë¡œ í•œ ê³„ê¸‰í™”: ë¯¸êµ­ vs í•œêµ­

### ë¯¸êµ­

* ë¸Œë£©ìŠ¤ëŠ” ê³ ë“±í•™êµ ì¡¸ì—…ê³¼ ëŒ€í•™ ì¡¸ì—… ì—¬ë¶€ê°€ í‰ìƒ ì†Œë“, ìˆ˜ëª…, ê±´ê°•, ê°€ì¡± ì•ˆì •ì„±, ì‚¬íšŒì  ê´€ê³„ë§ê¹Œì§€ ê°ˆë¼ë†“ëŠ”ë‹¤ê³  ì§€ì í•©ë‹ˆë‹¤.
* ì•„ì´ë¹„ë¦¬ê·¸ë¥¼ ë¹„ë¡¯í•œ ì†Œìˆ˜ ëª…ë¬¸ëŒ€ëŠ” ì‚¬ì‹¤ìƒ \*\*ê³„ê¸‰ì  ê´€ë¬¸(gate)\*\*ìœ¼ë¡œ ê¸°ëŠ¥í•˜ê³ , ì—¬ê¸°ì— ë“¤ì–´ê°€ëŠëƒ ì—¬ë¶€ê°€ ì¸ìƒì˜ ê¶¤ì ì„ ê²°ì •í•©ë‹ˆë‹¤.

### í•œêµ­

* í•œêµ­ ì—­ì‹œ **ëª…ë¬¸ëŒ€ ì¤‘ì‹¬ ì‚¬íšŒ**ì…ë‹ˆë‹¤. SKY(ì„œìš¸ëŒ€Â·ê³ ë ¤ëŒ€Â·ì—°ì„¸ëŒ€) ì§„í•™ ì—¬ë¶€ê°€ ì •ì¹˜Â·ì–¸ë¡ Â·ê²½ì œÂ·ë²•ì¡°Â·ì˜ë£Œ ì—˜ë¦¬íŠ¸ë¡œ ê°€ëŠ” ì£¼ ê²½ë¡œë¡œ ì‘ë™í•©ë‹ˆë‹¤.

## ê²°ë¡ 

ë¸Œë£©ìŠ¤ê°€ ë¹„íŒí•œ ë¯¸êµ­ì˜ ëŠ¥ë ¥ì£¼ì˜ì  ì¹´ìŠ¤íŠ¸í™”ëŠ” **êµìœ¡=ê³„ê¸‰**ì´ë¼ëŠ” ì ì—ì„œ í•œêµ­ ì‚¬íšŒì™€ ê¹Šê²Œ ë‹®ì•„ ìˆìŠµë‹ˆë‹¤.

ì›í•˜ì‹œë©´ ì œê°€ ì´ ë¹„êµ ë¶„ì„ì„ ë” í™•ì¥í•´ **â€œëŠ¥ë ¥ì£¼ì˜ ë¹„íŒì˜ ì² í•™ì  ê´€ì â€**(ì˜ˆ: ë§ˆì´í´ ìƒŒë¸, í•œë³‘ì² , U.G. í¬ë¦¬ìŠˆë‚˜ë¬´ë¥´í‹°)ê¹Œì§€ ì—°ê²°í•´ì„œ ì •ë¦¬í•´ë“œë¦´ ìˆ˜ë„ ìˆìŠµë‹ˆë‹¤. ê·¸ë ‡ê²Œ í•´ë“œë¦´ê¹Œìš”?




ì¢‹ìŠµë‹ˆë‹¤. ìš”ì²­í•˜ì‹  ëŒ€ë¡œ ë°ì´ë¹„ë“œ ë¸Œë£©ìŠ¤ê°€ ã€Œì•„ì´ë¹„ë¦¬ê·¸ê°€ ë¯¸êµ­ì„ ë§ì¹œ ë°©ì‹ã€ì—ì„œ **ì‚¬íšŒ ê³„ê¸‰í™”**(American caste system) ë¶€ë¶„ì„ ë¬˜ì‚¬í•œ ì›ë¬¸ì„ ê·¸ëŒ€ë¡œ ì œì‹œí•˜ê³ , ì´ì–´ì„œ ì œê°€ ì´ë¥¼ í•œêµ­ì–´ë¡œ ìƒì„¸íˆ í’€ì–´ ì„œìˆ í•´ë“œë¦¬ê² ìŠµë‹ˆë‹¤.

## ì›ë¬¸ (The Atlantic, David Brooks, *How the Ivy League Broke America*, 2024.12)

> **4. The meritocracy has created an American caste system. After decades of cognitive segregation, a chasm divides the well educated from the less well educated.**
>
> The average high-school graduate will earn about \$1 million less over their lifetime than the average four-year-college graduate. The average person without a four-year college degree lives about eight years less than the average four-year-college grad. Thirty-five percent of high-school graduates are obese, compared with 27 percent of four-year-college grads. High-school grads are much less likely to get married, and women with high-school degrees are about twice as likely to divorce within 10 years of marrying as women with college degrees. Nearly 60 percent of births to women with a high-school degree or less happen out of wedlock; thatâ€™s roughly five times higher than the rate for women with at least a bachelorâ€™s degree. The opioid death rate for those with a high-school degree is about 10 times higher than for those with at least a bachelorâ€™s degree.
>
> The most significant gap may be social. According to an American Enterprise Institute study, nearly a quarter of people with a high-school degree or less say they have no close friends, whereas only 10 percent of those with college degrees or more say that. Those whose education doesnâ€™t extend past high school spend less time in public spaces, less time in hobby groups and sports leagues. Theyâ€™re less likely to host friends and family in their home.
>
> The advantages of elite higher education compound over the generations. Affluent, well-educated parents marry each other and confer their advantages on their kids, who then go to fancy colleges and marry people like themselves. As in all caste societies, the segregation benefits the segregators. And as in all caste societies, the inequalities involve inequalities not just of wealth but of status and respect.
>
> The whole meritocracy is a system of segregation. Segregate your family into a fancy school district. If youâ€™re a valedictorian in Ohio, donâ€™t go to Ohio State; go to one of the coastal elite schools where all the smart rich kids are.
>
> It should be noted that this segregation by education tends to overlap with and contribute to segregation by race, a problem that is only deepening after affirmative actionâ€™s demise. Black people constitute about 14 percent of the U.S. population but only 9 percent of Princetonâ€™s current freshman class, according to the schoolâ€™s self-reported numbers, and only 3 percent of Amherstâ€™s and 4.7 percent of Tuftsâ€™s, according to federal reporting guidelines. (Princeton has declined to reveal what that number would be based on those federal guidelines.) In the year after the Supreme Court ended affirmative action, MIT says that the number of Black people in its freshman class dropped from 15 percent to 5 percent.
>
> For the past 50 years or so, the cognitive elite has been withdrawing from engagement with the rest of American society. Since about 1974, as the Harvard sociologist Theda Skocpol has noted, college-educated Americans have been leaving organizations, such as the Elks Lodge and the Kiwanis Club, where they might rub shoulders with non-educated-class people, and instead have been joining groups, such as the Sierra Club and the ACLU, that are dominated by highly educated folks like themselves.
>
> â€œWe now have a single route into a single dominant cognitive class,â€ the journalist David Goodhart has written. And because members of the educated class dominate media and culture, they possess the power of consecration, the power to determine what gets admired and what gets ignored or disdained. Goodhart notes further that over the past two decades, itâ€™s been as though â€œan enormous social vacuum cleaner has sucked up status from manual occupations, even skilled ones,â€ and reallocated that status to white-collar jobs, even low-level ones, in â€œprosperous metropolitan centers and university towns.â€ This has had terrible social and political consequences.

### 3. ì„¸ëŒ€ ê°„ ì¬ìƒì‚°

* ì—˜ë¦¬íŠ¸ ëŒ€í•™ ì¡¸ì—…ìë“¤ì€ ê°™ì€ ìˆ˜ì¤€ì˜ ë°°ìš°ìì™€ ê²°í˜¼í•˜ê³ , ìë…€ì—ê²Œë„ ê·¸ í˜œíƒì„ ë¬¼ë ¤ì¤€ë‹¤.

## 1. í”Œë¼ìŠ¤í‹± ê³µí•™ìì´ì ì˜ìš©ì†Œë°©ëŒ€ì› â€“ ë§ˆë¥´ì¿ ìŠ¤ ì••í ì˜ ì´ì¤‘ì  ì‚¶ê³¼ ì¹¼ ë©”ì¸ ì˜ ìœ ì‚°

ê·¸ì˜ í™œë™ì€ ì „ì‹œì¥ì˜ ë²½ë©´ì—ì„œë§Œ ì´ì–´ì§€ì§€ ì•Šì•˜ë‹¤. 2023ë…„ 6ì›”, ë§Œí•˜ì„ ì†Œë°©êµ­ 1978Â·1981ë…„ ì…ì‚¬ ë™ê¸°ë“¤ì˜ ëª¨ì„ì— ì´ˆì²­ëœ ê·¸ëŠ” 1ì°¨ ëŒ€ì „ ì´ì „ ë§Œí•˜ì„ ì†Œë°©ì˜ ì—­ì‚¬ë¥¼ ê°•ì—°í–ˆë‹¤. ì§€í˜•ê³¼ ë¬¼ê¸¸, ì¥ë¹„ì˜ ê³„ë³´ì™€ ì¡°ì§ì˜ ë³€ì²œì„ ë”ë“¬ëŠ” ê·¸ì˜ ì–¸ì–´ëŠ”, ì‚¬ê±´ê³¼ ì œë„ê°€ ì–´ë–»ê²Œ ì„œë¡œë¥¼ ë§Œë“¤ì—ˆëŠ”ì§€ ë³´ì—¬ì£¼ëŠ” ì§€ë„ì˜ ì—­í• ì„ í–ˆë‹¤. ê·¸ ê°•ì—° ì´í›„ ëª‡ëª‡ì€ í˜‘íšŒì— ê°€ì…í–ˆë‹¤. 2024ë…„, í¬ì´ë´í•˜ì„ ì†Œë°©ì„œ 150ì£¼ë…„ì„ ë§ì•„ ê·¸ëŠ” ê¸‰í•œ ì¼ì • ì†ì—ì„œë„ 16ì¥ì˜ ëŒ€í˜• ì‚¬ì§„ íŒ¨ë„ì„ ë§Œë“¤ì–´ ì—­ì‚¬ ì „ì‹œë¥¼ ì„¸ì› ë‹¤. ê·¸ë‚ , ê·¸ëŠ” ê¸°ë¡ì˜ ì†ì´ì—ˆê³ , ì—°ì¶œì˜ ì†ì´ì—ˆë‹¤. ê·¸ë¦¬ê³  ê³µë™ì²´ì˜ ì†ì´ì—ˆë‹¤.

ì—¬ê¸°ì„œ ìºë¡œì‹ ì„œì‚¬ì˜ í•µì‹¬ì´ ëª¨ìŠµì„ ë“œëŸ¬ë‚¸ë‹¤. ê°œì¸ì˜ ì„±ì‹¤ì€ í˜¼ì ì‘ë™í•˜ì§€ ì•ŠëŠ”ë‹¤. ê·¸ê²ƒì€ ì œë„ì™€ ì¥ë¹„, ì¥ì†Œì™€ ê¸°ì–µì´ ì–½íŒ êµ¬ì¡° ì†ì—ì„œ ì˜ë¯¸ë¥¼ ì–»ëŠ”ë‹¤. ì••í ì´ í–ˆë˜ í™˜ìˆ˜Â·ë³µì›Â·ì „ì‹œëŠ”, ì¥ë¹„ë¥¼ êµ¬í•˜ëŠ” í–‰ë™ì´ì ì œë„ë¥¼ ì„¸ìš°ëŠ” í–‰ë™ì´ì—ˆë‹¤. ëª¨ê¸ˆê³¼ ì„¤ë“, í˜‘íšŒì˜ ì°½ë¦½, ìƒì„¤ ì „ì‹œ ê³µê°„ì˜ í™•ë³´, ëŒ€ì¤‘ êµìœ¡ê³¼ ê°•ì—°â€”ì´ ëª¨ë“  ê³¼ì •ì€ í•œ ë„ì‹œê°€ ìê¸° ìì‹ ì„ ê¸°ì–µí•˜ëŠ” ì œë„ë¥¼ ë‹¤ì‹œ ì„¸ìš°ëŠ” ì¼ì´ì—ˆë‹¤. ê·¸ê°€ ê°•ë‹¨ì—ì„œ í”Œë¼ìŠ¤í‹± ê³µì •ì„ ê°€ë¥´ì¹  ë•Œì™€, ì „ì‹œì¥ ì•ì—ì„œ ì†Œë°©ì—­ì‚¬ë¥¼ ì„¤ëª…í•  ë•Œ, ê·¸ëŠ” ì„œë¡œ ë‹¤ë¥¸ ë„ì‹ì„ ê°™ì€ ëª©ì†Œë¦¬ë¡œ ì½ëŠ”ë‹¤. ë°˜ë³µê³¼ ê²€ì¦, ê¸°ì¤€ê³¼ ê³„ìŠ¹. ì´ê²ƒì´ ê·¸ê°€ ë¯¿ëŠ” ì§ˆì„œì´ê³ , ê·¸ ì§ˆì„œëŠ” ì‚¬ëŒì„ ì‚´ë¦°ë‹¤.

ê·¸ë˜ì„œ ì´ ì´ì•¼ê¸°ëŠ” ê²°êµ­ í•œ ë¬¸ì¥ìœ¼ë¡œ ì¢í˜€ì§„ë‹¤. ë§ˆë¥´ì¿ ìŠ¤ ì••í ì€ ë¶ˆê³¼ í”Œë¼ìŠ¤í‹± ì‚¬ì´ì—ì„œ, ì¹¼ ë©”ì¸ ì˜ ê¿ˆì„ í˜„ì¬í˜•ìœ¼ë¡œ ë²ˆì—­í•œ ì‚¬ëŒì´ë‹¤. ë‚®ì˜ ì‹¤í—˜ì‹¤ì—ì„œ ê·¸ëŠ” ì¬ë£Œì˜ ì§ˆì„œë¥¼ ê°€ë¥´ì¹˜ê³ , ë°¤ì˜ ì°¨ê³ ì—ì„œ ê·¸ëŠ” ê³µë™ì²´ì˜ ì§ˆì„œë¥¼ ì§€í‚¨ë‹¤. 150ë…„ ì „ ì¥ë¹„ì™€ ì¡°ì§ìœ¼ë¡œ ë„ì‹œì˜ ìƒëª…ì„ ë¶™ë“¤ë˜ ë©”ì¸ ì˜ ì •ì‹ ì€, ê·¸ì˜ ì†ì—ì„œ ê³ ë¶„ìì™€ ë³µí•©ì¬, êµìœ¡ê³¼ ì „ì‹œë¼ëŠ” ì˜¤ëŠ˜ì˜ ì–¸ì–´ë¡œ ë‹¤ì‹œ ì‚´ì•„ë‚œë‹¤. í•œ ì‚¬ëŒì˜ ì´ì¤‘ì  ì‚¶ì´ í•œ ë„ì‹œì˜ ì˜¤ë˜ëœ ì§ˆì„œë¥¼ ë˜ì‚´ë¦¬ê³ , ê·¸ ì§ˆì„œê°€ ë‹¤ì‹œ ì‚¬ëŒë“¤ì˜ ì•ˆì „ê³¼ ìë¶€ì‹¬ì„ ë– ë°›ì¹œë‹¤. ì´ ì—°ì‡„ì•¼ë§ë¡œ ìš°ë¦¬ê°€ â€˜ìœ ì‚°â€™ì´ë¼ ë¶€ë¥´ëŠ” ê²ƒì˜ ì‹¤ì²´ë‹¤. ê·¸ë¦¬ê³  ì••í ì€ ê·¸ ìœ ì‚°ì˜ ê°€ì¥ ì„¤ë“ë ¥ ìˆëŠ” ì¦ì¸ì´ë‹¤.

## 2. ê´´ë¸Â·ì—ì…”Â·ë°”íì˜ ë¯¸í•™ê³¼ ë…ì¼ì •ì‹  â€“ ì¹¼ ë©”ì¸ ì™€ ë¹„ìŠ¤ë§ˆë¥´í¬ì˜ ë³‘ì¹˜, ê·¸ë¦¬ê³  í”Œë¼ìŠ¤í‹± ì‚°ì—…ì˜ ê¸°ì 

í•œ ì¥ì˜ ì—ì…” íŒí™”ì²˜ëŸ¼ ì‹œì‘ëœ ì´ì•¼ê¸°â€”ëì´ ì‹œì‘ì„ ë¶€ë¥´ê³ , ì‹œì‘ì´ ë‹¤ì‹œ ëìœ¼ë¡œ ì ‘íˆëŠ” ë¬´í•œ íƒ€ì¼ë§. ì¹¼ ë©”ì¸ ì˜ ìƒì• ë¥¼ ê·¸ë ‡ê²Œ ì½ì„ ë•Œ ë¹„ë¡œì†Œ ê·¸ì˜ ìœ„ëŒ€í•¨ì€ ê¸°ìˆ ìì˜ ì†ì¬ì£¼ë‚˜ ì°½ì„¤ìì˜ ê¸°ì§€ì— ë¨¸ë¬¼ì§€ ì•Šê³ , ì§ˆì„œê°€ ìŠ¤ìŠ¤ë¡œë¥¼ ë‚³ëŠ” êµ¬ì¡°ì˜ ë¯¸í•™ìœ¼ë¡œ ë– ì˜¤ë¥¸ë‹¤. ë¶ˆì´ë¼ëŠ” í˜¼ëˆì„ ë‹¤ë£¨ëŠ” ì‘ì—…ì€ ì–¸ì œë‚˜ ë°˜ë³µì„ ì „ì œë¡œ í•œë‹¤. ì¥ë¹„ëŠ” ì ê²€ë˜ê³ , í›ˆë ¨ì€ ë˜í’€ì´ë˜ë©°, ê¸°ì–µì€ ì „ìˆ˜ëœë‹¤. ì¥ë¹„â€“í›ˆë ¨â€“ê¸°ì–µì˜ 3ì„±ë¶€ê°€ ì„œë¡œì˜ ì„ ìœ¨ì„ ë°›ì•„ì¹œë‹¤. ì–´ëŠ ë‚ ì˜ ì¶œë™ì´ ë‹¤ìŒ ë‚ ì˜ ë§¤ë‰´ì–¼ì„ ê³ ì¹˜ê³ , ê·¸ ë§¤ë‰´ì–¼ì´ ë‹¤ì‹œ ì¥ë¹„ì˜ í‘œì¤€ì„ ë°”ê¾¸ë©°, í‘œì¤€ì´ ë‹¤ì‹œ ê³µë™ì²´ì˜ ì‹ ë¢°ë¥¼ ë§Œë“ ë‹¤. ì—ì…”ì˜ ë°˜ë³µì²˜ëŸ¼ ë³€í•¨ì—†ì§€ë§Œ, ë°”íì˜ í‘¸ê°€ì²˜ëŸ¼ ëŠ˜ ë‹¤ë¥´ê²Œ ì‘ë‹µí•˜ëŠ” ìˆœí™˜. ë©”ì¸ ì˜ ì˜ìš©ì†Œë°©ëŒ€ëŠ” ë°”ë¡œ ê·¸ êµ¬ì¡°ë¡œ ì‘ë™í–ˆê³ , ê·¸ êµ¬ì¡°ê°€ ì§€ì—­ì˜ ìƒëª…ì„ ì§€íƒ±í–ˆë‹¤.

ê´´ë¸ì˜ ì •ë¦¬ëŠ” ê±°ê¸°ì— ë§ë¶™ì¸ë‹¤. ì™„ê²°ì€ ì—†ë‹¤. ì ˆëŒ€ ì•ˆì „ì€ ì¡´ì¬í•˜ì§€ ì•Šìœ¼ë©°, ì²´ê³„ëŠ” ìŠ¤ìŠ¤ë¡œë¥¼ ë³´ì¦í•˜ëŠ” ë¬¸ì¥ì„ ëë‚´ ê°–ì§€ ëª»í•œë‹¤. ê·¸ëŸ¬ë‚˜ ë°”ë¡œ ê·¸ë ‡ê¸°ì— ì²´ê³„ëŠ” ìŠ¤ìŠ¤ë¡œë¥¼ ê°±ì‹ í•˜ë©° ë²„í‹´ë‹¤. ì˜¤ëŠ˜ì˜ ì ê²€í‘œëŠ” ë‚´ì¼ì˜ ê· ì—´ì„ ë°œê²¬í•  ì—¬ì§€ë¥¼ ë‚¨ê¸°ê³ , ê·¸ ì—¬ì§€ê°€ ì œë„ë¥¼ ì›€ì§ì¸ë‹¤. ë©”ì¸ ì˜ ìœ„ëŒ€í•¨ì€ â€˜ì™„ì„±â€™ì— ìˆì§€ ì•Šì•˜ë‹¤. ë¶ˆì™„ì „ì„±ì„ ì œë„í™”í•˜ëŠ” ì‹¤ìš©ì  í˜•ì‹â€”ë°˜ë³µÂ·ê¸°ë¡Â·ê°œì„ â€”ì„ ê³µë™ì²´ì˜ ìŠµê´€ìœ¼ë¡œ ë§Œë“  ë° ìˆì—ˆë‹¤. ì—ì…”ì  ë°˜ë³µê³¼ ê´´ë¸ì  ë¶ˆì™„ì „ì„±ì˜ ê¸´ì¥ ìœ„ì—ì„œ, ë°”íì  í•©ì£¼ê°€ ê³µë™ì²´ë¥¼ í•˜ë‚˜ë¡œ ë¬¶ëŠ”ë‹¤.

ê²°êµ­, ë©”ì¸ ì˜ ê¸°ìˆ ê³¼ ê³µë™ì²´, ë¹„ìŠ¤ë§ˆë¥´í¬ì˜ ì œë„ì™€ ê¶Œë ¥, ë¼ì¸ê°• ê¸°ì ì˜ ì‚°ì—…ê³¼ ë¶„ì—…ì´ GEBì˜ ì‚¼ì¤‘ ë¯¸í•™ìœ¼ë¡œ ì„œë¡œë¥¼ ë¹„ì¶°ì¤„ ë•Œ, ê·¸ ê±°ìš¸ ì†ì—ì„œ ìš°ë¦¬ëŠ” ì˜¤ëŠ˜ì˜ ë…ì¼ì •ì‹ ì„ ë³¸ë‹¤. ê·¸ë¦¬ê³  ê·¸ ì •ì‹ ì€ ì¶”ìƒì  ê¸°ì¹˜ê°€ ì•„ë‹ˆë¼ ì†ì˜ ìŠµê´€ì´ë‹¤. ë¶ˆì™„ì „ì„±ì„ ì¸ì •í•˜ê³ â€”ê´´ë¸â€”, ë°˜ë³µì„ ì œë„í™”í•˜ë©°â€”ì—ì…”â€”, ì„œë¡œ ë‹¤ë¥¸ ì„±ë¶€ê°€ í•˜ë‚˜ì˜ ìŒì•…ì„ ì´ë£¨ê²Œ ì¡°ìœ¨í•˜ëŠ”â€”ë°”íâ€”ìŠµê´€. ë§ˆë¥´ì¿ ìŠ¤ ì••í ì´ ê³¼í•™ìì˜ ì‹¤í—˜ëŒ€ì™€ ì†Œë°©ëŒ€ì›ì˜ ì¥ë¹„ ì°¨ê³ ë¥¼ ì˜¤ê°€ë©° ë³´ì—¬ì¤€ ê²ƒì€ ë°”ë¡œ ê·¸ ìŠµê´€ì˜ í˜„ëŒ€ì  ì „ë²”ì´ì—ˆë‹¤. í”Œë¼ìŠ¤í‹± ì‚°ì—…ì´ ë…ì¼ì„ ë¶€ìœ í•˜ê²Œ ë§Œë“  ê²ƒì€ ë¬¼ì§ˆì´ ì‹¸ê³  ê°€ë³ê¸° ë•Œë¬¸ë§Œì´ ì•„ë‹ˆë‹¤. ê·¸ ë¬¼ì§ˆì„ ë‹¤ë£¨ëŠ” ì†ë“¤ì´ ì—ì…”Â·ê´´ë¸Â·ë°”íì˜ ë¬¸ë²•ì„ ì¼ìƒìœ¼ë¡œ ì²´í™”í–ˆê¸° ë•Œë¬¸ì´ë‹¤. í•œ ë„ì‹œì˜ ì—­ì‚¬ì™€ í•œ ì‚¬ëŒì˜ ì‚¶ì´ ê·¸ ë¬¸ë²•ì„ ì¦ì–¸í•œë‹¤. ê·¸ë¦¬ê³  ê·¸ ì¦ì–¸ì€ ë‚´ì¼ë„ ë°˜ë³µë  ê²ƒì´ë‹¤â€”ì™„ê²°ì€ ì—†ì§€ë§Œ, í•©ì£¼ëŠ” ê³„ì†ëœë‹¤.

ë…ì¼ì´ ì§€ë‚œ ì„¸ê¸° ë§ë¶€í„° 2000ë…„ëŒ€ ì¤‘ë°˜ê¹Œì§€ ê¸°êº¼ì´ ê°ìˆ˜í–ˆë˜ í™˜ëŒ€ì˜ ìœ¤ë¦¬ëŠ”, ì² í•™ê³¼ ì‹ í•™ì˜ ì˜¤ë˜ëœ ë¿Œë¦¬ë¥¼ ê°–ê³  ìˆì—ˆë‹¤. ì¹¸íŠ¸ì˜ ì •ì–¸ëª…ë ¹â€”íƒ€ìë¥¼ ë‹¨ì§€ ìˆ˜ë‹¨ì´ ì•„ë‹ˆë¼ ëª©ì ìœ¼ë¡œ ëŒ€í•˜ë¼ëŠ” ìš”êµ¬â€”ì™€ ê·¸ë¦¬ìŠ¤ë„êµì  ì¸ë³¸ì£¼ì˜ëŠ” ì •ì¹˜ì˜ ì–¸ì–´ë¡œ ë²ˆì—­ë  ë•Œ ë‚œë¯¼ê³¼ ì´ë¯¼ìë¥¼ ëŒì–´ì•ˆëŠ” ê³µì  ê°ì •ì˜ ë¬¸ë²•ì´ ë˜ì—ˆë‹¤. ê·¸ ë¬¸ë²•ì€ 2015ë…„ ìœ ëŸ½ ë‚œë¯¼ ìœ„ê¸°ì˜ ì •ì ì—ì„œ ë…ì¼ì˜ êµ­ê²½ì„ ì—¬ëŠ” ì„ íƒìœ¼ë¡œ êµ¬ì²´í™”ëë‹¤. ë‹¹ì‹œ ë…ì¼ ì •ë¶€ëŠ” ì‹œë¦¬ì•„ì¸ë“¤ì— ëŒ€í•´ ë”ë¸”ë¦° ê·œì •ì˜ ì§‘í–‰ì„ ì‚¬ì‹¤ìƒ ì •ì§€í•˜ë©° ë…ì¼ì—ì„œì˜ ë‚œë¯¼ ì‹ ì²­ì„ í—ˆìš©í–ˆê³ , ì´ ê²°ì •ì€ ê¸‰ì†íˆ í™•ì‚°ë¼ ë…ì¼ì´ ì‹œë¦¬ì•„ì¸ ë“± ë³´í˜¸ ìš”ì²­ìë“¤ì˜ 1ì°¨ ëª©ì ì§€ë¡œ ë¶€ìƒí•˜ëŠ” ê³„ê¸°ê°€ ë˜ì—ˆë‹¤. ì´ë“¬í•´ê¹Œì§€ ë…ì¼ì€ ì•½ ë°±ë§Œ ëª… ê·œëª¨ì˜ ë³´í˜¸ ì‹ ì²­Â·ë„ì°©ì„ ê²½í—˜í•œë‹¤. ë…ì¼ ì—°ë°©ì´ì£¼ë‚œë¯¼ì²­(BAMF)ì€ 2015ë…„ 47ë§Œ 6ì²œ ê±´ì˜ ë‚œë¯¼ ì‹ ì²­ì´ ë“±ë¡ë˜ì—ˆê³ , ì´ˆê¸° ë¶„ë°°(EASY) ì‹œìŠ¤í…œì—ëŠ” ì¤‘ë³µì„ í¬í•¨í•œ ì•½ 110ë§Œ ëª…ì˜ ë„ì°©ì´ ì¡í˜”ë‹¤ê³  ì •ë¦¬í•œë‹¤. ì´í›„ 2016ë…„ì—ëŠ” ì „ë…„ ë¯¸ë“±ë¡ë¶„ê¹Œì§€ ì²˜ë¦¬ë˜ë©° ì‹ ì²­ ê±´ìˆ˜ê°€ 74ë§Œ 5ì²œ ê±´ìœ¼ë¡œ ê¸‰ì¦í–ˆë‹¤. ì´ ì¼ë ¨ì˜ ìˆ˜ì¹˜ëŠ” ë…ì¼ì´ ì—¬ëŠ” í™˜ëŒ€ì˜ ìœ¤ë¦¬ê°€ ê°ë‹¹í•´ì•¼ í•  ì¬ì •Â·í–‰ì •Â·ì‚¬íšŒì  ë¹„ìš©ì˜ í¬ê¸°ë¥¼ ê°ê´€ì ìœ¼ë¡œ ë§í•´ì¤€ë‹¤. ([case.hks.harvard.edu][1], [bamf.de][2], [ecre.org][3])

ê·¸ëŸ¬ë‚˜ ì—­ì‚¬ëŠ” ê°ì •ì˜ ë°˜ì „ìœ¼ë¡œ ì „ì§„í•œë‹¤. 2015ë…„ 12ì›” 31ì¼, ì¾°ë¥¸ ì¤‘ì•™ì—­ ì¼ëŒ€ì—ì„œ ë²Œì–´ì§„ ëŒ€ê·œëª¨ ì„±ë²”ì£„Â·ì ˆë„ ì‚¬ê±´ì€ ì´ í™˜ëŒ€ì˜ ì—ë¡œìŠ¤ë¥¼ ê·¼ë³¸ì—ì„œ í”ë“¤ì—ˆë‹¤. ë‹¤ìŒ ë‚ ë¶€í„° ë…ì¼ ì „ì—­ì˜ ì–¸ë¡ ê³¼ ìˆ˜ì‚¬ë‹¹êµ­ì€ ìˆ˜ë°± ê±´ì˜ ë²”ì£„ ì‹ ê³ ë¥¼ ì§‘ê³„í–ˆê³ , ìˆ˜ì‚¬ì™€ ë…¼ìŸì´ ë’¤ì–½íˆëŠ” ì‚¬ì´ì— ì—¬ë¡ ì€ ê¸‰ì†íˆ ìš”ë™ì³¤ë‹¤. ë…ì¼Â·ì˜êµ­Â·ë¯¸êµ­ ì£¼ìš” ë§¤ì²´ë“¤ì˜ ì·¨ì¬ëŠ” ì‚¬ê±´ì˜ ì‹¬ê°ì„±ê³¼ ì •ì¹˜ì  íŒŒì¥ì„ í¬ì°©í–ˆê³ , ë©”ë¥´ì¼ˆ ì •ë¶€ì˜ ê°œë°© ì •ì±…ì€ ì •ë©´ìœ¼ë¡œ ë¹„íŒì˜ ëŒ€ìƒì´ ë˜ì—ˆë‹¤. êµ­ê°€ê°€ ì—¬ì„±ì˜ ì•ˆì „ê³¼ ê³µê³µ ì§ˆì„œë¥¼ ì¶©ë¶„íˆ ì§€í‚¤ì§€ ëª»í–ˆì„ ë•Œ, í™˜ëŒ€ì˜ ìœ¤ë¦¬ëŠ” ì–´ë–¤ ì´ë¦„ìœ¼ë¡œ ê·¸ ì±…ì„ì„ ê°ë‹¹í•˜ëŠ”ê°€â€”ê·¸ë‚  ë°¤ ì´í›„ ë…ì¼ ì‚¬íšŒê°€ ë§¤ì¼ ë˜ì§„ ì§ˆë¬¸ì´ì—ˆë‹¤. ([ê°€ë””ì–¸][4], [TIME][5], [Der Spiegel][6])

í•œë³‘ì² ì€ ì´ ê²©ë‘ì„ ì„¤ëª…í•˜ëŠ” ë” ê¹Šì€ ì–´íœ˜ë¥¼ ì œê³µí•œë‹¤. ê·¸ì—ê²Œ ì—ë¡œìŠ¤ëŠ” â€˜íƒ€ìì˜ ì¶œí˜„â€™ ì—†ì´ëŠ” ì„±ë¦½í•  ìˆ˜ ì—†ë‹¤. ì‚¬ë‘ì€ ìê¸°í™•ì¥ì˜ ë‹¤ë¥¸ ì´ë¦„ì´ ì•„ë‹ˆë¼, ìê¸°ì˜ ì ì •ì  ì†Œê±°â€”ìê¸° ë¶€ì •â€”ë¥¼ ê°ìˆ˜í•˜ê³  íƒ€ìë¥¼ ë°›ì•„ë“¤ì´ëŠ” ìš©ê¸°ë‹¤. í˜„ëŒ€ì˜ ë””ì§€í„¸-ì„±ê³¼ì£¼ì˜ëŠ” ì´ íƒ€ìì„±ì˜ ê±°ì¹ ê³  ê³ í†µìŠ¤ëŸ¬ìš´ ë§ˆì£¼ì¹¨ì„ íšŒí”¼í•˜ê²Œ ë§Œë“ ë‹¤. íƒ€ìë¥¼ ë°ìš°ìŠ¤ ë§ˆí‚¤ë‚˜ì²˜ëŸ¼ í˜¸ì¶œí•˜ê³  ì‚­ì œí•˜ëŠ” UI ìœ„ì—ì„œ, ì—ë¡œìŠ¤ëŠ” ì„œì„œíˆ ì§ˆì‹í•œë‹¤. ë…ì¼ì˜ í™˜ëŒ€ëŠ” ì´ ì² í•™ì  ê¸´ì¥ì„ êµ­ê°€ ê·œëª¨ë¡œ ê°ë‹¹í•˜ë ¤ëŠ” ì‹œë„ì˜€ë‹¤. íƒ€ìë¥¼ ì§„ì§œë¡œ ë§ì•„ë“¤ì´ë ¤ë©´, ë°°ì¹˜ì™€ ë¹„ìš©ê³¼ ì‹œê°„ì´ í•„ìš”í•˜ë‹¤. ê·¸ëŸ°ë° ì¾°ë¥¸ì˜ ë°¤ì€, ê·¸ ê³ í†µì˜ ê°ë‚´ê°€ ê³µë™ì²´ì˜ ì•ˆì „ê³¼ ì‹ ë¢°ë¼ëŠ” ë” ê·¼ë³¸ì ì¸ ì§ˆì„œì™€ ì¶©ëŒí•  ìˆ˜ ìˆìŒì„ ë“œëŸ¬ëƒˆë‹¤. ê·¸ë•Œ ì—ë¡œìŠ¤ëŠ” ë°°ì‹ ìœ¼ë¡œ ì½í˜”ë‹¤. ê·¸ë¦¬ê³  í™˜ëŒ€ì˜ ìœ¤ë¦¬ì™€ ì§ˆì„œì˜ ìœ¤ë¦¬ ì‚¬ì´ì—ì„œ, ë…ì¼ì€ ë‹¤ì‹œ ê²°ì •ì„ ë‚´ë ¤ì•¼ í–ˆë‹¤. ([MIT Press][7])

ì •ì¹˜ì˜ ê³„ê¸°ëŠ” ë¹ ë¥´ê²Œ ì œë„ ì–¸ì–´ë¡œ ë²ˆì—­ëœë‹¤. ë…ì¼ì€ í™˜ëŒ€ì˜ ìŠ¤ìœ„ì¹˜ë¥¼ ì™„ì „íˆ ë‚´ë¦¬ì§€ ì•Šì•˜ì§€ë§Œ, ê²½ê³„ì™€ ì ˆì°¨, ì§ˆì„œì™€ ìƒí˜¸ ì˜ë¬´ì˜ ì–¸ì–´ë¥¼ ê°•í™”í–ˆë‹¤. ë°”ë¡œ ì´ ì§€ì ì—ì„œ AfDì˜ ê·€í™˜ì€ ë‹¨ìˆœí•œ ë°˜ì‚¬ ì‹ ê²½ì´ ì•„ë‹ˆë¼, ì‚¬ìƒì  ì „í™˜ì˜ í‘œì‹œë¡œ ì½íŒë‹¤. AfDì˜ ê°•ë ¹ì€ êµ­ë¯¼íˆ¬í‘œ í™•ëŒ€, ìŠ¬ë¦¼í•œ ì •ë¶€, ê¶Œë ¥ë¶„ë¦½ ê°•í™”, êµ­ê²½ ê´€ë¦¬ì™€ ì´ë¯¼ ê·œìœ¨, EU ê¶Œí•œì˜ ë³µê·€ ë“±â€”êµ­ê°€ì˜ ê¸°ë³¸ ê·œì¹™ì„ ë‹¤ì‹œ ì„ ëª…íˆ í•˜ìëŠ” ì¼ë ¨ì˜ ì¡°í•­ë“¤ë¡œ êµ¬ì„±ë¼ ìˆë‹¤. ì´ëŠ” í™˜ëŒ€ì˜ ìœ¤ë¦¬ë¥¼ íê¸°í•˜ìëŠ” êµ¬í˜¸ê°€ ì•„ë‹ˆë¼, í™˜ëŒ€ê°€ ì„±ë¦½í•  ê³µì  í‹€â€”ë²•ê³¼ ê·œì¹™, ì¬ì •ê³¼ í–‰ì •ì˜ ëŠ¥ë ¥â€”ì„ ìš°ì„ ì ìœ¼ë¡œ ë³µì›í•˜ìëŠ” ì£¼ì¥ìœ¼ë¡œ í•´ì„ë  ìˆ˜ ìˆë‹¤. ([Alternative fÃ¼r Deutschland][8], [DIE WELT][9])

ì´ ì „í™˜ì„ í”„ë¦¬ë“œë¦¬íˆ í•˜ì´ì—í¬ì˜ ê´€ì ì—ì„œ ë³´ë©´, AfDì˜ ëª‡ëª‡ í•µì‹¬ ì£¼ì¥ì€ ê¸ì •ì  í•¨ì˜ë¥¼ ê°–ëŠ”ë‹¤. í•˜ì´ì—í¬ì—ê²Œ ì¢‹ì€ ì •ë¶€ëŠ” íŠ¹ì •í•œ ì‹¤ì²´ì  ê²°ê³¼ë¥¼ â€˜ì„¤ê³„â€™í•˜ëŠ” ê¸°ê´€ì´ ì•„ë‹ˆë¼, ììƒì  ì§ˆì„œê°€ ì‘ë™í•  ìˆ˜ ìˆë„ë¡ ì•ˆì •ì ì´ê³  ì¼ë°˜ì ì¸ ê·œì¹™ì˜ í‹€ì„ ì œê³µí•˜ëŠ” ê¸°ê´€ì´ë‹¤. ì§€ì‹ì€ ì¤‘ì•™ì— ëª¨ì´ì§€ ì•Šìœ¼ë©°, êµ­ê²½ì˜ ì•ˆíŒ, ì§€ë°©ê³¼ ë„ì‹œ, ì‚°ì—…ê³¼ ê³µë™ì²´ì— í©ì–´ì§„ ì±„ë¡œ í˜„ì¥ì—ì„œ ì‚´ì•„ ì›€ì§ì¸ë‹¤. ë”°ë¼ì„œ ììœ ì˜ ì§ˆì„œëŠ” ê³„íšëœ ëª©í‘œê°€ ì•„ë‹ˆë¼, ê·œì¹™ê³¼ ì±…ì„Â·ì†Œìœ Â·ê³„ì•½ì´ ë³´ì¥ëœ ì¥ì—ì„œ ì‹œë¯¼ê³¼ ê¸°ì—…, ì§€ë°©ê³¼ ë¯¼ê°„ì´ ìŠ¤ìŠ¤ë¡œ ì¡°ì •í•˜ë©° ë°œê²¬í•´ë‚´ëŠ” ê²°ê³¼ë‹¤. ì´ ê´€ì ì—ì„œ ë³´ë©´, êµ­ê²½Â·ì‹œë¯¼ê¶ŒÂ·ë³µì§€ ì ‘ê·¼ì˜ ê·œì¹™ì„ ë¶„ëª…íˆ í•˜ê³ , EU ìˆ˜ì¤€ì˜ ì¼ê´„ ì„¤ê³„ë¥¼ ê²½ê³„í•˜ë©°, ì¬ì •Â·ì¹˜ì•ˆÂ·ë™í™” ì—­ëŸ‰ì„ ëƒ‰ì •í•˜ê²Œ ë”°ì ¸ ì´ë¯¼ì˜ ì†ë„ì™€ ê·œëª¨ë¥¼ ì¡°ì •í•˜ìëŠ” ìš”êµ¬ëŠ” â€˜ì‚¬íšŒê³µí•™ì˜ ì•¼ì‹¬â€™ì„ ì¤„ì´ê³  â€˜ê·œì¹™ì˜ í”„ë ˆì„â€™ì„ ë³µì›í•˜ë ¤ëŠ” ì¶©ë™ìœ¼ë¡œ ì½íŒë‹¤. í™˜ëŒ€ë¥¼ ê°€ëŠ¥ì¼€ í•˜ëŠ” ê²ƒë„ ê²°êµ­ ì´ í”„ë ˆì„ì´ë‹¤. ([plato.stanford.edu][10])

í•˜ì´ì—í¬ê°€ ê°•ì¡°í•œ ë˜ í•˜ë‚˜ì˜ í¬ì¸íŠ¸ëŠ” ê·œì¹™ ì¼ë°˜ì„±ê³¼ ì˜ˆì¸¡ê°€ëŠ¥ì„±ì´ë‹¤. ì‹œë¯¼ì´ ì˜¤ëŠ˜ì˜ ì„ íƒì„ ë‚´ì¼ë„ ìœ íš¨í•œ ê·œì¹™ ì•„ë˜ì„œ í•  ìˆ˜ ìˆì–´ì•¼, ì‹œì¥ê³¼ ì‹œë¯¼ì‚¬íšŒê°€ ì¥ê¸° íˆ¬ìë¥¼ í•œë‹¤. ë©”ë¥´ì¼ˆ ì‹œê¸°ì˜ ì‹ ì†í•œ ê·œì¹™ ë³€í™”â€”ë”ë¸”ë¦° ê·œì • ì ìš©ì˜ ì •ì§€, ëŒ€ê·œëª¨ ë„ì°©ê³¼ ì‚¬í›„ì  í–‰ì • ì²˜ë¦¬â€”ëŠ” ìœ„ê¸° êµ­ë©´ì˜ ë¶ˆê°€í”¼í•œ ì„ íƒì´ì—ˆì§€ë§Œ, ê²°ê³¼ì ìœ¼ë¡œ ì§€ë°©ìì¹˜ë‹¨ì²´Â·ê²½ì°°Â·ë²•ì›Â·ë³µì§€í–‰ì •Â·êµìœ¡ í˜„ì¥ì— ì˜ˆì¸¡ ë¶ˆê°€ëŠ¥ì„±ì„ ë†’ì˜€ê³ , ì´ëŠ” í™˜ëŒ€ì˜ ì§€ì†ê°€ëŠ¥ì„±ì„ ì ì‹í–ˆë‹¤. AfDì‹ì˜ â€˜ê·œì¹™ íšŒë³µâ€™ ë‹´ë¡ ì€ ì´ ë¶ˆí™•ì‹¤ì„±ì„ ë‚®ì¶”ë ¤ëŠ” ì‹œë„ë¡œ, í•˜ì´ì—í¬ì˜ ë²•ì¹˜ ê°œë…â€”íŠ¹ì • ê²°ê³¼ê°€ ì•„ë‹ˆë¼ â€˜ì ˆì°¨â€™ì˜ ì•ˆì •â€”ì™€ ìƒì‘í•œë‹¤. í™˜ëŒ€ê°€ ì œë„í™”ë˜ê¸° ìœ„í•´ì„œëŠ” ê·œì¹™ê³¼ ì¸ì„¼í‹°ë¸Œ, ì±…ì„ì˜ ê²½ê³„ê°€ ë‹¤ì‹œ ê·¸ë ¤ì ¸ì•¼ í•œë‹¤. ([Oxford Law Blogs][11])

ë¬¼ë¡  AfDì˜ ì–¸ì–´ëŠ” ë…ì¼ ê³µë¡ ì¥ì—ì„œ ê²©ë ¬í•œ ë…¼ìŸì„ ë¶€ë¥¸ë‹¤. ê·¸ëŸ¬ë‚˜ í•˜ì´ì—í¬ì  ì²™ë„â€”ì§€ì‹ì˜ ë¶„ì‚°, ììƒì  ì§ˆì„œ, ë²•ì˜ ì¼ë°˜ì„±, ì •ì¹˜ì  ê¶Œë ¥ì˜ ìê¸°ì ˆì œâ€”ë¡œ ì ê²€í•˜ë©´, ìµœì†Œí•œ ì„¸ ê°€ì§€ ì¥ì ì´ ë˜ë ·í•˜ë‹¤. ì²«ì§¸, ì¤‘ì•™ì§‘ì¤‘ì  ì„¤ê³„ë¥¼ ê²½ê³„í•˜ê³  ë³´ì¡°ì„± ì›ì¹™ì— ë”°ë¼ ê¶Œí•œì„ ì§€ë°©ê³¼ êµ­ê°€ë¡œ í™˜ë¥˜ì‹œí‚¤ë ¤ëŠ” ì‹œë„. ì´ëŠ” í˜„ì¥ ì§€ì‹ì´ í’ë¶€í•œ ë‹¨ìœ„ë¡œ ë¬¸ì œ í•´ê²°ì˜ ê¶Œí•œì„ ë‚´ë ¤ë³´ë‚´ëŠ” ê²ƒìœ¼ë¡œ, í•˜ì´ì—í¬ê°€ ë§í•œ ë°œê²¬ ì ˆì°¨ë¥¼ ì´‰ì§„í•œë‹¤. ë‘˜ì§¸, êµ­ê²½ê³¼ ì‹œë¯¼ê¶Œ ê·œì¹™ì˜ ëª…í™•í™”. ë¶ˆëª…í™•í•œ ì‹ í˜¸ëŠ” ì˜ëª»ëœ ìœ ì¸ì„ ë‚³ê³ , ì˜ëª»ëœ ìœ ì¸ì€ ì¢‹ì€ ì˜ë„ë¥¼ íŒŒê´´í•œë‹¤. ê·œì¹™ì„ ë¶„ëª…íˆ í•¨ìœ¼ë¡œì¨ â€˜ëˆ„êµ¬ë¥¼, ì–¸ì œ, ì–´ë–»ê²Œâ€™ ë•ëŠ”ì§€ì˜ ì‚¬íšŒì  í•©ì˜ë¥¼ ì¬êµ¬ì¶•í•  ìˆ˜ ìˆë‹¤. ì…‹ì§¸, ì¬ì •ì˜ ì§€ì†ê°€ëŠ¥ì„±. í™˜ëŒ€ëŠ” ë¹„ìš©ì˜ ë‹¤ë¥¸ ì´ë¦„ì´ë©°, ë¹„ìš©ì€ ì˜ì§€ì™€ ëŠ¥ë ¥ì´ ê²°í•©ë  ë•Œë§Œ ì§€ì†ëœë‹¤. í•˜ì´ì—í¬ì‹ ë²•ì¹˜ê°€ ì§€í–¥í•˜ëŠ” ê²ƒë„ ë°”ë¡œ ì´ â€˜ëŠ¥ë ¥ì— ë§ì¶˜ ê·œì¹™â€™ì´ë‹¤. ([Alternative fÃ¼r Deutschland][8], [DIE WELT][9], [plato.stanford.edu][10])

ì—¬ê¸°ì„œ ë‹¤ì‹œ ì¾°ë¥¸ìœ¼ë¡œ ëŒì•„ê°€ ë³´ì. ì‚¬ê±´ ì§í›„ ë…ì¼ì˜ ê±°ë¦¬ì—ëŠ” ì—¬ì„±ì˜ ê¶Œë¦¬ë¥¼ ì™¸ì¹˜ëŠ” ì§‘íšŒì™€ ë°˜ë‚œë¯¼ ì‹œìœ„ë¥¼ ë§‰ìœ¼ë ¤ëŠ” ê²½ì°°ì˜ ìµœë£¨ê°€ìŠ¤, ìƒì²˜ ì…ì€ í™˜ëŒ€ì˜ ì–¸ì–´ê°€ ë™ì‹œì— ë’¤ì—‰ì¼°ë‹¤. ì‹œë¯¼ì  ììœ ì™€ ê³µê³µ ì•ˆì „, í™˜ëŒ€ì™€ ê²½ê³„ëŠ” ì„œë¡œë¥¼ ë°°ì œí•˜ëŠ” í‚¤ì›Œë“œê°€ ì•„ë‹ˆë‹¤. í•˜ì´ì—í¬ê°€ ë§í•˜ë“¯, ììœ ëŠ” ê³µí—ˆí•œ êµ¬í˜¸ê°€ ì•„ë‹ˆë¼ ê·œì¹™ ì†ì˜ ì§ˆì„œë‹¤. ê·œì¹™ì´ ì•½í™”ë ìˆ˜ë¡ ììœ ëŠ” ê´‘ì¥ì— ì˜¤ë˜ ë¨¸ë¬¼ ìˆ˜ ì—†ë‹¤. ê·¸ë‚ ì˜ êµí›ˆì„ â€˜ë°°ì‹ â€™ì´ë¼ëŠ” ê°ì •ìœ¼ë¡œë§Œ ë‚¨ê¸¸ ê²ƒì¸ê°€, ì•„ë‹ˆë©´ ê·œì¹™ì˜ ì¬ì„¤ê³„ë¥¼ í†µí•´ í™˜ëŒ€ì™€ ì•ˆì „ì„ í•¨ê»˜ ì‘ë™ì‹œí‚¤ëŠ” ë” ê°•í•œ í”„ë ˆì„ìœ¼ë¡œ ìŠ¹í™”ì‹œí‚¬ ê²ƒì¸ê°€â€”AfDì˜ ë“±ì¥ì€ ì´ ì§ˆë¬¸ì— ëŒ€í•œ í•˜ë‚˜ì˜ ë‹µì´ì—ˆë‹¤. ê·¸ë¦¬ê³  ê·¸ ë‹µì€, ë…ì¼ì´ ì˜¤ë«ë™ì•ˆ ë°œì „ì‹œì¼œì˜¨ ì§ˆì„œ ììœ ì£¼ì˜ì™€ë„ ì ‘ì ì´ ìˆë‹¤. ì¹¼ ë©”ì¸ ì˜ ê³µë™ì²´ ì´ìƒì€ ê·¸ ë¬¸ì¥ì„ ë§ˆì„ì˜ ìŠ¤ì¼€ì¼ë¡œ ë²ˆì—­í•œë‹¤. ê·¸ëŠ” ë„ì‹œê°€ ë¶ˆê³¼ ë¬¼ë¡œ ì‹œí—˜ë°›ë˜ ì‹œëŒ€ì—, ì¥ë¹„ì™€ ì¡°ì§, í›ˆë ¨ê³¼ ê¸°ë¡ìœ¼ë¡œ ë§ˆì„ì˜ ìƒëª…ì„ ë¶™ì¡ì•˜ë‹¤. ê·¸ì˜ ì˜ìš©ì†Œë°©ëŒ€ëŠ” â€˜ì¢‹ì€ ì˜ì§€â€™ê°€ ì•„ë‹ˆë¼ â€˜ì¢‹ì€ ì ˆì°¨â€™ì˜ ì´í•©ì´ì—ˆë‹¤. ë¬´ì§ˆì„œë¥¼ ì„ ì˜ë¡œ ì´ê¸¸ ìˆ˜ ì—†ë‹¤ëŠ” ì‚¬ì‹¤, ë”°ë¼ì„œ ì„ ì˜ë¥¼ ì ˆì°¨ì™€ ì¥ë¹„ì˜ ì–¸ì–´ë¡œ ë°”ê¿”ì•¼ í•œë‹¤ëŠ” ì‚¬ì‹¤ì„ ëª¸ìœ¼ë¡œ ê°€ë¥´ì³¤ë‹¤. ë©”ì¸ ê°€ ë³´ì—¬ì¤€ ê²ƒì€ ì‘ì€ êµ­ê°€ì˜ ëª¨í˜•ì´ë‹¤. ì§€íœ˜ ì²´ê³„ì™€ ë¶„ì—…, í‘œì¤€ê³¼ ì ê²€, ì±…ì„ê³¼ ì—°ëŒ€ê°€ í•œ ëª¸ì²˜ëŸ¼ ì‘ë™í•˜ëŠ” í˜„ì¥. ê·¸ í˜„ì¥ì—ì„œ í™˜ëŒ€ëŠ” ê²½ê³„ ìœ„ì—ì„œë§Œ ì„±ë¦½í•˜ê³ , ì—°ëŒ€ëŠ” ì±…ì„ê³¼ í•¨ê»˜ë§Œ ì§€ì†ëœë‹¤.

ì´ì œ ì„¸ ì¸µìœ„ê°€ ê²¹ì¹œë‹¤. ë¹„ìŠ¤ë§ˆë¥´í¬ì˜ ì œêµ­ì  ì„¤ê³„, í•˜ì´ë°ê±°ì˜ ì¡´ì¬ë¡ ì  ì¥ì†Œ, ë©”ì¸ ì˜ ë§ˆì„ì  ì§ˆì„œ. ê·œëª¨ëŠ” ë‹¤ë¥´ì§€ë§Œ ë°©í–¥ì€ ê°™ë‹¤. ê³µë™ì²´ì˜ ìê¸°ë³´ì¡´ì„ ë„ë•ì˜ ì–¸ì–´ê°€ ì•„ë‹ˆë¼ ì œë„ì™€ ì ˆì°¨ì˜ ì–¸ì–´ë¡œ ë³´ì¦í•˜ë ¤ëŠ” ì˜ì§€. ê·€í™˜ì€ ì´ ê²¹ì¹¨ì„ ì¬í™•ì¸í•˜ëŠ” ì¼ì´ë‹¤. êµ­ê°€ê°€ ë‹¤ì‹œ ì¥ì†Œê°€ ë˜ê³ , ì œë„ê°€ ë‹¤ì‹œ ì˜ë¯¸ë¥¼ ê°–ê³ , ê³µë™ì²´ê°€ ë‹¤ì‹œ ì±…ì„ì˜ ë¬¶ìŒìœ¼ë¡œ ì´í•´ë˜ëŠ” ìˆœê°„â€”ê·¸ë•Œ í™˜ëŒ€ëŠ” ë¹„ë¡œì†Œ ì§€ì†ê°€ëŠ¥í•´ì§„ë‹¤. ê²½ê³„ê°€ ìˆì–´ì•¼ ë¬¸ì´ ëœë‹¤. ë¬¸ì´ ìˆì–´ì•¼ ì•ˆê³¼ ë°–ì´ ë¶„ëª…í•´ì§€ê³ , ì•ˆê³¼ ë°–ì´ ë¶„ëª…í•´ì•¼ ë§Œë‚¨ì´ ì§„ì§œê°€ ëœë‹¤. ê·€í™˜ì€ ë°°ì œì˜ ì„ ì–¸ì´ ì•„ë‹ˆë¼ ë§Œë‚¨ì„ ì§„ì§œë¡œ ë§Œë“¤ë ¤ëŠ” ì¤€ë¹„ë‹¤.
